Does Matter's Eternity Entail Its Self-Necessity?
Ghazali questions whether matter's inability to be created or annihilated entails its self-necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that if matter is eternal (neither created from nothing nor annihilated), it would possess the characteristics of necessary existence, challenging the philosophers' position.

Core Idea
Eternal matter implies self-necessity, contradicting contingency.

In this passage, Ghazali poses a critical challenge to those who claim matter is eternal. He argues that if matter cannot be created or annihilated, it takes on the attributes of necessary existence. This forces a dilemma: either accept matter's self-necessity or reconcile eternity with contingency. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the two premises
Notice the premises: matter cannot arise from nothing, and matter cannot become nothing. These are the foundation of Ghazali's reasoning.
3
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that matter would then have the properties (خصائص) of self-necessity (وجوب ذاتي). Ask yourself: why would that follow?
A necessary being is defined as one that cannot not exist and cannot be caused.
4
Consider the rhetorical questions
Ghazali asks whether proponents commit to self-necessity and whether one can hold both eternity and contingency. Reflect on the tension.
5
Reflect on implications
Think about how this argument undermines the philosophers' claim that matter is eternal yet contingent. What would a defender need to resolve?
The passage ends with a double question mark, indicating the difficulty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا كانت المادة لا يمكن ولا يعقل ، أن تصير إلى عدم ، ولا يمكن ولا يعقل أن تنشأ من عدم، أليست تكون قد استكملت خصائص الوجوب الذاتي ؟ فهل يلتزم القول بالوجوب الذاتى للمادة ، أولئك الذين يقولون : إن المادة لا توجد في عدم ، ولا تصير إلى عدم ؟ وهل يمكن الجمع بين القول : بأن المادة لا توجد من عدم ، ولا تصير إلى عدم ، و بين القول : بأن المادة في ذاتها ممكنة ، لا توجد إلا إذا وجدت أسباب وجودها ، ولا تعدم إلا إذا تحققت أسباب عدمها إذا كان القول : بأن المادة لا توجد من عدم ، ولا تصير إلى عدم ، يستلزم القول بوجوب المادة وجوباً ذاتياً ؟ ! !
Then, if matter cannot and is inconceivable to become nothing, and cannot and is inconceivable to arise from nothing, does it not have the properties of self-necessity? So do those who say that matter is not found in nothing nor becomes nothing commit to the statement of the self-necessity of matter? And is it possible to combine the statement that matter does not arise from nothing nor become nothing with the statement that matter is in itself contingent, existing only when the causes of its existence exist, and not ceasing except when the causes of its ceasing are realized? If the statement that matter does not arise from nothing nor become nothing entails the statement of the self-necessity of matter? ! !
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a challenging question that Ghazali poses to those who hold that matter is eternal (i.e., never created from nothing nor annihilated). He argues that if matter has these two properties—incapability of creation from nothing and incapability of annihilation—then it would possess the characteristics of necessary existence (wujūb dhātī). He then asks whether such proponents are willing to commit to the self-necessity of matter. He further asks if one can consistently maintain both that matter is eternal (not created from nothing, not annihilated) and that matter is contingent in itself (mumkin bi-dhātihā), depending on external causes for existence and non-existence. The implication is that eternal uncreated matter would contradict contingency, because a contingent being is supposed to be able to not exist, but if it cannot be annihilated, that seems to imply necessity. The passage ends with a double rhetorical question, leaving the difficulty for defenders of matter's eternity to resolve.

Key Terms
مادة
وجوب ذاتي
إمكان
عدم
أسباب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that if matter is eternal, it is automatically necessary in itself without further reasoning.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the two properties (non-creation, non-annihilation) are characteristics of necessary existence, but one could argue that contingency is compatible if matter depends on causes. However, the inability to not exist seems to contradict contingency.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali imply about matter if it cannot be created from nothing and cannot be annihilated?
A) It is contingent in itself.
B) It is necessary in itself.
C) It requires a cause for its existence.
D) It is a creation of God.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that the eternity of matter (non-creation and non-annihilation) logically implies that matter possesses the attributes of necessary existence, creating a tension with the claim that matter is contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If matter were eternal and self-necessary, it would not need a Creator. Do you ever live as if something in your life—wealth, relationships, or health—is permanent and self-sufficient, independent of Allah? What would it take for you to truly internalize that only Allah is necessary?
?
Ghazali shows that claiming matter is eternal leads to a logical contradiction with contingency. Where in your own beliefs do you hold ideas that, if followed to their end, would contradict tawheed? What step can you take today to align your heart fully with the oneness of Allah?
?
The impossibility of matter's non-existence would imply necessity. Is there anything or anyone you assume will always be there for you, as if they were necessary? How does that assumption affect your reliance on Allah, and what can you do to shift that reliance exclusively to Him?

Ghazali's Argument on Matter and Necessary Existence
Ghazali argues that if matter is not from nothing and not to nothing, it would have necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical argument exposing the tension between the philosophical view of matter's eternity and its claimed contingency.

Core Idea
If matter cannot be created or destroyed, it is necessary, not possible.

In this passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali forces the reader to confront a logical consequence of the philosophers' view that matter is eternal. If matter never begins and never ends, it shares essential attributes of necessary existence. Ghazali then questions whether this can be reconciled with the claim that matter is contingent and dependent on causes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the passage and identify the two claims about matter that Ghazali presents: it does not come from nothing and does not become nothing.
This is the starting point of the argument.
2
Recognize the implication
Notice that Ghazali immediately connects these two attributes to the characteristics of necessary existence (wajib al-wujud).
3
Identify the question
Ghazali asks whether the proponents of the view must accept that matter has necessary existence. This is the key challenge.
4
Examine the tension
Consider the follow-up question: how can one say matter is possible in itself while also holding it is uncreated and indestructible? The latter seems to entail necessity.
This reveals a contradiction in the philosophical position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا كانت المادة لا يمكن ولا يعقل، أن تصير إلى عدم، ولا يمكن ولا يعقل أن تنشأ من عدم، أليست تكون قد استكملت خصائص الوجوب الذاتي ؟ فهل يلتزم القول بالوجوب الذاتى للمادة ، أولئك الذين يقولون : إن المادة لا توجد في عدم ، ولا تصير إلى عدم ؟ وهل يمكن الجمع بين القول : بأن المادة لا توجد من عدم ، ولا تصير إلى عدم ، و بين القول : بأن المادة في ذاتها ممكنة ، لا توجد إلا إذا وجدت أسباب وجودها ، ولا تعدم إلا إذا تحققت أسباب عدمها إذا كان القول : بأن المادة لا توجد من عدم ، ولا تصير إلى عدم ، يستلزم القول بوجوب المادة وجوباً ذاتياً ؟ ! !
Then, if matter cannot and is inconceivably not to become nothing, and cannot and is inconceivably not to arise from nothing, has it not then completed the characteristics of necessary existence? So do those who say that matter does not exist in nothingness and does not become nothingness commit themselves to saying that matter has necessary existence? And is it possible to combine the statement that matter does not arise from nothing and does not become nothing, with the statement that matter is possible in itself, not existing except when its causes of existence exist, and not perishing except when its causes of perishing are realized, if the statement that matter does not arise from nothing and does not become nothing entails the statement that matter has necessary existence?
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents a logical implication: if matter cannot originate from nothing and cannot cease into nothing, then it shares two key attributes of necessary existence (eternal persistence and no beginning). Ghazali then asks whether the proponents of the philosophers' view (that matter is not created from nothing and not annihilated) accept that matter is necessary. He then raises a further question: can one reconcile the claim that matter is possible in itself (dependent on causes) with the claim that it is not from nothing and not to nothing, since the latter seems to imply necessity? The passage thus exposes a tension in the philosophical position.

Key Terms
الوجوب الذاتي
المادة
ممكن في ذاتها
أسباب الوجود
أسباب العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that saying matter is eternal is simply a description without implying necessity.
Correction: Ghazali argues that eternality (no origin and no end) are exactly the defining attributes of necessary existence, so claiming matter is eternal logically entails that it is necessary, contradicting its being contingent.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali suggest is a consequence of saying matter does not arise from nothing and does not become nothing?
A) That matter is created by God.
B) That matter has necessary existence.
C) That matter is composed of atoms.
D) That matter is purely mental.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's argument shows that the philosophers' claim of matter's eternality logically entails necessary existence, contradicting their assertion that matter is contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the universe, do you ever assume it just 'is' and always has been, as if it were necessary? Where in your heart do you rely on the world's permanence rather than on Allah? Commit today to whisper 'Ya Qayyum' whenever you catch yourself treating the world as self-sufficient.
?
If matter were truly eternal, it would deserve worship as a necessary being. Do you ever unconsciously elevate anything created—money, health, relationships—to the level of the unneeded, as if it could not be taken away? What would it mean for you to truly see everything as contingent, held by Allah every moment? Choose one object of your attachment and say: 'You are possible, Allah alone is necessary.'
?
Ghazali exposes a contradiction in those who claim matter is both eternal and contingent. Are there contradictions in your own beliefs—times you intellectually affirm Allah's sole necessity but live as if causes are ultimate? What is one area of your life where you act as if the means are necessary rather than dependent? Resolve to remind yourself before that action: 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah.'

Ghazali's Rational Proof for the World's Temporal Creation
Ghazali presents a rational argument for the world's temporal creation by a pre-eternal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's rational argument that the world was created by a pre-eternal will at a specific moment.

Core Idea
A pre-eternal will can choose when creation begins.

In this passage, Ghazali steps back from his critique of the philosophers to present his own rational argument for the world's temporal origination. He signals that he will use reason alone, just as Ibn Sina did, but to prove the opposite conclusion. He then outlines a coherent model: a pre-eternal will that determines the exact moment of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's method statement
Notice that Ghazali says he too will rely on reason alone, like Ibn Sina, but to argue for the temporal creation of the world.
This shows Ghazali is not anti-reason; he just disagrees with the philosophers' conclusions.
2
Identify the core model
Find the four elements: pre-eternal will, continued non-existence, existence beginning at a specific time, and the will attaching to that time.
3
Understand the challenge
Ghazali asks what is impossible about this model. This is a direct challenge to the philosophers to provide a rational proof against it.
The burden of proof is on the philosophers to show why such a model is impossible.
4
Reflect on the role of will
Consider how a pre-eternal will can choose one time over another without changing. Ghazali argues that will itself is sufficient to explain the timing.
This introduces a key concept: the will does not need a prior reason for its choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : إذا كان ابن سينا يسلك هذا المسلك العقلى ، غير مبال بما عسى أن يكون هناك من نصوص دينية ، تفيد بظاهرها ، حدوث العالم ؛ فإن الغزالي يعول أيضاً على العقل وحده في إثبات حدوث العالم فيقول مخاطباً ابن سينا والفارابي :

بم تنكرون على من يقول : إن العالم حدث بإرادة قديمة اقتضت وجوده في الوقت الذي وجد فيه، وأن يستمر العدم إلى الغاية التي استمر إليها ، وأن يبتدئ الوجود من حيث ابتدأ ، وأن الوجود قبله لم يكن مراداً ، فلم يحدث لذلك، وأنه في وقته الذى حدث فيه مراد بالإرادة القديمة ، فحدث لذلك ، فما المانع هذا الاعتقاد وما المحيل له ؟
I say: Since Ibn Sīnā follows this rational path, unconcerned with whatever religious texts might be there that in their apparent meaning indicate the temporal creation of the world, al-Ghazālī also relies on reason alone to prove the world's temporal creation, saying—addressing Ibn Sīnā and al-Fārābī: 'Why do you reject one who says: The world originated through a pre-eternal will that necessitated its existence at the time it came to be, and that non-existence continued up to the limit to which it continued, and existence began from where it began, and that before that existence was not willed, so it did not originate, and that at the time it originated it was willed by the pre-eternal will, so it originated accordingly. So what prevents this belief and what renders it impossible?'
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage contains Ghazali's own rational argument for the world's temporal creation. He begins by noting that Ibn Sina relies solely on reason, disregarding religious texts that suggest creation. Ghazali then announces that he too will rely on reason alone, but to prove the opposite: that the world is temporally originated. He directly challenges Ibn Sina and al-Farabi by presenting a coherent model: the world was created by a pre-eternal will that determined its existence at a specific moment. Non-existence persisted until that moment, then existence began because the will attached to that time. Ghazali asks what is impossible about this belief, demanding a rational proof. This sets up the subsequent objection and answer.

Key Terms
إرادة قديمة (pre-eternal will)
حدوث العالم (temporal creation of the world)
العقل (reason)
النصوص الدينية (religious texts)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali rejects reason and relies only on religious texts.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly relies on reason alone in this argument, challenging the philosophers on their own terms.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's argument, what causes the world to exist at a particular time?
A) A pre-eternal will that chooses that time
B) The nature of the cause
C) A temporal decision
D) The absence of impediments

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves that a pre-eternal will can rationally explain the world's temporal origin without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's existence is not necessary but a choice of a pre-eternal will, what does that say about the significance of your own existence? Are you living as if your life is a deliberate gift or a random accident? Write down one specific way you will acknowledge the will of the Creator in your daily routine starting tomorrow.
?
Ghazali asks, 'What prevents this belief?' After reading his argument, what prevents you from fully submitting to the idea that everything in your life—including disappointments and delays—is under the control of a pre-eternal will? Identify one area you still try to control yourself and commit to surrendering it today.
?
Consider the phrase 'before that existence was not willed, so it did not originate.' Reflect on a missed opportunity or a failure in your life. Can you accept that it was not willed for you? How does that change your attitude toward disappointment? What lesson can you draw from this? Resolve to see unfulfilled wishes as a sign of divine wisdom rather than personal failure.

The Absence of Renewal in the Divine Agent
Ibn Sina asserts the constancy of the divine state absent renewal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina argues that the absence of renewal in God implies a single continuous state, and how this challenges arguments for a specific moment of creation.

Core Idea
No renewal in God means no reason to pick one moment over another.

Ibn Sina begins his defense by setting up a crucial premise: if God's nature is unchanging, then any delay in creation cannot be due to a change in God. This passage examines the implications of a constant divine state for the temporal origination of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how Ibn Sina emphasizes the uniformity of the divine state.
The passage uses conditional phrasing 'if there is no renewal...' to introduce the premise.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage claims that if there is no renewal, then the state is one continuous state. Identify what 'renewal' (تجدد) refers to here.
Renewal here means any change in God's will or attributes.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this argument targets the Ash'ari view that God creates the world at a specific time. How does a constant state undermine the idea of a particular moment of creation?
This sets the stage for the next part of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و إذا لم يكن تجدد ، كانت حال ما لم يتجدد شيء ، حالا واحدة مستمرة على نهج واحد و سواء جعلت التجدد لأمر تيسر ، أو لأمر زال ، مثلا ، كحسن من الفعل ، وقت ما تيسر ، أو معين ، أو غير ذلك ، مما عد ، كان عد ، أو كقبح يكون له ، أو كان قد زال ، أو عائق ، أو غير ذلك كان فزال . فإن كان الداعى إلى تعطيل واجب الوجود عن إفاضة الخير والجود ؛ هو كون المعلول مسبوق العدم لا محالة ، فهذا الداعي ضعيف قد انكشف لذوى الإنصاف ضعفه ، على أنه قائم فى كل حال ، ليس في حال أولى بإيجاب السبق من حال . وأما كون المعلول ممكن الوجود فى نفسه ، واجب الوجود بغيره فليس يناقض كونه دائم الوجود بغيره (۱)
And if there is no renewal, the state of that which is not renewed is one continuous state, on a single course. And it is the same whether you make the renewal due to a matter that has become easy, or due to a matter that has ceased, for example, like the goodness of an act at the time it became easy, or an aid, or other such things, whether they are counted or not, or like an evil that belongs to it, or had ceased, or an obstacle, or other such thing that had ceased. For if the motive for preventing the Necessary Existent from bestowing good and generosity is that the effect is necessarily preceded by non-existence, then this motive is weak, and its weakness has become clear to those of fairness, despite it being present in every state, that no state is more deserving of necessitating precedence than another. And as for the effect being possible in itself and necessary through another, that does not contradict its being perpetual through another.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Ibn Sina's al-Isharat (as indicated by the footnote). It presents the philosophers' defense of the claim that the world, though temporally originated in one sense, is eternal through God. The argument addresses the problem of why the world was created at a particular time. Ibn Sina begins by stating that if there is no renewal (تجدد) in the Agent, then the state of the Agent remains unchanged. Whether the renewal is attributed to something that becomes easy or something that ceases, the situation is symmetrical. He then considers the possible motive (داعي) for God delaying the bestowal of existence: that the effect must be preceded by non-existence. Ibn Sina claims this motive is weak because it would apply equally at all times, so no specific moment is more appropriate than another. Finally, he asserts that the effect being possible (ممكن) in itself and necessary (واجب) through another does not contradict its being perpetual through that other. In other words, the world's contingency does not preclude its eternal existence when sustained by God.

Key Terms
تجدد
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will is subject to renewal or change over time.
Correction: Ibn Sina argues that if God were subject to renewal, His state would not be constant; but since He is unchanging, the state is one and continuous, no moment is special.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina in this passage, what is the state of that which is not renewed?
A) Subject to change
B) One continuous state
C) Dependent on external factors
D) Nonexistent

Key Takeaway
The absence of renewal in the divine agent implies that no moment is inherently different from another, undermining arguments for a specific temporal beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's state is entirely unchanging, then your prayers, your repentance, and your good deeds do not cause Him to 'react' or 'change His mind'. Does this free you to worship Him without ulterior motives, or does it feel unsettling that He remains unaffected by your actions? How will you adjust your worship to focus purely on His glory rather than on what you gain?
?
The text argues that without renewal in God, there is no reason to prefer one moment over another. Reflect on your own delays: are you postponing a difficult act of obedience thinking that 'any time will do'? How does the eternal, uniform nature of God urge you to act now instead of later? What specific step will you take today?
?
Ibn Sina's argument touches on the idea that God's will is eternal and unchanging. If God willed your salvation or guidance from eternity, then every good in your life is a result of that eternal decree. How does this truth confront your pride when you achieve something good? Write down one way you can humbly thank God for an eternal plan, not just a recent blessing.

The Weakness of the Motive for Delaying Creation
Ibn Sina weakens the motive for temporal origination and asserts the perpetual existence of the effect through the Necessary Existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's claim that the motive for delaying creation is weak and constant, and how this leads to the conclusion that the effect can be perpetual through another.

Core Idea
The motive for delay is equally weak at every moment, so the effect is perpetual through God.

Having established the constancy of the divine state, Ibn Sina now addresses the possible motive for delaying the bestowal of existence. He argues that if the worry is the effect's precedence by non-existence, that worry is present always and thus cannot justify choosing one moment over another. He then concludes with a crucial distinction between the effect's own possibility and its necessary existence through God.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the conditional 'فإن كان الداعى...' (if the motive is...).
This is the core of Ibn Sina's defense.
2
Identify the motive being rejected
The motive is that the effect is necessarily preceded by non-existence. Ibn Sina says this motive is weak. Why?
The motive is weak because it is constant across all times.
3
Understand the conclusion
Ibn Sina ends with a key distinction: the effect is possible in itself but necessary through another, and this does not contradict its perpetual existence through that other.
This shows that contingency does not require temporal origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و إذا لم يكن تجدد ، كانت حال ما لم يتجدد شيء ، حالا واحدة مستمرة على نهج واحد و سواء جعلت التجدد لأمر تيسر ، أو لأمر زال ، مثلا ، كحسن من الفعل ، وقت ما تيسر ، أو معين ، أو غير ذلك ، مما عد ، كان عد ، أو كقبح يكون له ، أو كان قد زال ، أو عائق ، أو غير ذلك كان فزال . فإن كان الداعى إلى تعطيل واجب الوجود عن إفاضة الخير والجود ؛ هو كون المعلول مسبوق العدم لا محالة ، فهذا الداعي ضعيف قد انكشف لذوى الإنصاف ضعفه ، على أنه قائم فى كل حال ، ليس في حال أولى بإيجاب السبق من حال . وأما كون المعلول ممكن الوجود فى نفسه ، واجب الوجود بغيره فليس يناقض كونه دائم الوجود بغيره (۱)
And if there is no renewal, the state of that which is not renewed is one continuous state, on a single course. And it is the same whether you make the renewal due to a matter that has become easy, or due to a matter that has ceased, for example, like the goodness of an act at the time it became easy, or an aid, or other such things, whether they are counted or not, or like an evil that belongs to it, or had ceased, or an obstacle, or other such thing that had ceased. For if the motive for preventing the Necessary Existent from bestowing good and generosity is that the effect is necessarily preceded by non-existence, then this motive is weak, and its weakness has become clear to those of fairness, despite it being present in every state, that no state is more deserving of necessitating precedence than another. And as for the effect being possible in itself and necessary through another, that does not contradict its being perpetual through another.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Ibn Sina's al-Isharat (as indicated by the footnote). It presents the philosophers' defense of the claim that the world, though temporally originated in one sense, is eternal through God. The argument addresses the problem of why the world was created at a particular time. Ibn Sina begins by stating that if there is no renewal (تجدد) in the Agent, then the state of the Agent remains unchanged. Whether the renewal is attributed to something that becomes easy or something that ceases, the situation is symmetrical. He then considers the possible motive (داعي) for God delaying the bestowal of existence: that the effect must be preceded by non-existence. Ibn Sina claims this motive is weak because it would apply equally at all times, so no specific moment is more appropriate than another. Finally, he asserts that the effect being possible (ممكن) in itself and necessary (واجب) through another does not contradict its being perpetual through that other. In other words, the world's contingency does not preclude its eternal existence when sustained by God.

Key Terms
داعي
معلول
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود
إفاضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A contingent being must have a beginning in time.
Correction: Ibn Sina argues that a being that is possible in itself and necessary through another can be perpetual through that cause; contingency does not necessitate temporal origination.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, why is the motive for preventing God from bestowing existence weak?
A) Because it is based on a misunderstanding of divine generosity
B) Because it is present in every state and not stronger at any one time
C) Because the effect's non-existence is not real
D) Because God's will is arbitrary

Key Takeaway
The motive for delaying creation is constant and weak, so no specific moment of creation can be justified; the effect can be perpetual through God despite its contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own life, do you sometimes feel that God is delaying an answer to your prayers? Ibn Sina argues that the motive for God's action is constant and weak. Is it possible that your perception of delay comes from your own timetable, not from a lack of God's readiness? What would it mean for you to trust that God's goodness is always equally present, even when you don't see immediate results?
?
Ibn Sina says the effect is 'possible in itself and necessary through another.' Reflect: do you live as though your existence is merely a given, or do you feel its ongoing dependency on God every moment? How might your daily awareness change if you truly believed that without God's continuous support you would cease to exist?
?
The passage suggests that the motive for creation is the same at every moment, so no moment is special for creation. Are there areas of your life where you are waiting for a 'special moment' to act—a sign, a feeling, or a perfect circumstance? How does Ibn Sina's reasoning challenge the idea that some moments are more 'blessed' than others for starting something good? What holds you back from acting now?

The Problem of the Preponderator: First Formulation
Ghazali presents the first of three proofs against direct emanation from eternal to temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first formulation of the problem of the preponderator and why it shows that a temporal event cannot proceed directly from an eternal being.

Core Idea
A new preponderating factor simply pushes the question back; it does not solve the problem.

Ghazali begins his critique of the philosophers by condensing their argument into a precise thesis: no temporal event can emanate from an eternal without an intermediary. He then offers multiple interpretations, each serving as an independent proof. In this first formulation, he presents a logical dilemma that forces the philosophers into an infinite regress or a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Pay attention to the opening thesis and the dilemma that follows.
The brackets indicate direct quotes from Ghazali's own words.
2
Identify the thesis
Locate Ghazali's statement: 'It is impossible for a temporal event to emanate from the eternal without any intermediary at all.' This is the core claim he will defend.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Notice the two horns: either a new preponderating factor (مرجح) arises or it does not. Consider the consequence of each horn as Ghazali explains.
The dilemma is a standard kalam argument structure.
4
Reflect on the infinite regress
If a new factor arises, we must ask who caused it and why now. This leads to an endless chain. Understand why this is problematic for the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اقرأ الغزالى ، واعجب كيف ركز دليلهم في هذه العبارة الوجيزة الجامعة : يستحيل صدور حادث من قديم بغير واسطة أصلا ] ثم راح يشرحها شروحاً مختلفة يعتبر كل واحد منها كأنه دليل قائم بنفسه ، فيقول : أولاً : [ إنا إذا فرضنا القديم ولم يصدر منه العالم مثلاً، فإنما لم يصدر لأنه لم يكن للوجود مرجح ، بل كان وجود العالم ممكناً إمكاناً صرفاً فإذا حدث بعد ذلك لم يخل : إما أن يكون قد تجدد مرجح أو لم يتجدد فإذا لم يتجدد مرجح ، بقى العالم على الإمكان الصرف ، كما كان قبل ذلك . و إن تجدد مرجح ، فمن محدث ذلك المرجح ؟ ولم حدث الآن ولم يحدث من قبل ؟ والسؤال في حدوث المرجح قائم ]
Then read al-Ghazali, and marvel at how he condensed their proof in this brief comprehensive statement: 'It is impossible for a temporal event to emanate from the eternal without any intermediary at all.' Then he proceeded to explain it with various explanations, each of which he considers as an independent proof. He says: First: If we suppose the eternal and the world, for example, has not issued from it, then it did not issue only because there was no preponderating factor for existence, but rather the existence of the world was pure possibility. Then when it occurs afterwards, it is inevitable: either a new preponderating factor has arisen, or not. If no new preponderating factor has arisen, the world remains in pure possibility, as it was before. And if a new preponderating factor has arisen, then who originated that preponderating factor? And why did it occur now and not before? The question regarding the occurrence of the preponderating factor stands.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This lesson presents Ghazali's introduction to the philosophers' argument and his first formulation of it. The passage begins by quoting the core thesis: 'It is impossible for a temporal event to emanate from the eternal without any intermediary at all.' Ghazali then explains that he will provide multiple interpretations, each functioning as an independent proof. The first interpretation uses a dilemma: Suppose the eternal (God) and the world did not exist from eternity. The world's existence was merely possible (ممكن إمكاناً صرفاً) without a preponderating factor (مرجح). When it later comes into existence, either a new preponderating factor arises or not. If not, the world remains in pure possibility—so no change occurs. If a new factor does arise, that factor itself requires an explanation: who originated it, and why now rather than before? The same question recurs for that factor, leading to an infinite regress. This shows that the original thesis—that a temporal event can issue from an eternal being—is problematic.

Key Terms
حادث
قديم
واسطة
مرجح
الإمكان الصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about whether the universe was created ex nihilo or from pre-existing matter.
Correction: The argument is about the logical impossibility of a temporal first event proceeding from an immutable eternal being without a mediating cause.

Checkpoint
In Ghazali's first formulation, what happens if no new preponderating factor (مرجح) arises when the world comes into existence?
A) The world remains in pure possibility.
B) The world comes into existence by itself.
C) The preponderating factor is assumed to be eternal.
D) The argument becomes invalid.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first formulation exposes a logical flaw: any new factor introduced to explain the first event itself requires a cause, leading to an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own life, do you ever push the question 'why did I do that?' back to a first intention? What does Ghazali's argument reveal about your inability to ultimately justify your own actions without reference to the Eternal?
?
If every new factor in your life requires a preceding cause, how does that chain end? Are you comfortable leaving it in infinite regress, or does this argument force you to acknowledge a First Cause that is not itself caused?
?
In moments of pride, do you act as if you are self-sufficient? How does the realization that your existence depends on a preponderating factor beyond yourself change how you view every success or failure?

The Indistinguishability of States
Ghazali gives the second proof: if the eternal's states are uniform, there can be no distinction between inaction and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second argument that the eternity of the world follows from the uniformity of the eternal's states.

Core Idea
Uniform states leave no room for a temporal beginning.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a concise but powerful argument. He reasons that if the eternal's states are indistinguishable, then there can be no basis for choosing between inaction and action at a particular moment. This leads to a dilemma: either nothing ever happens or it happens perpetually, making a temporal beginning impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Note the key terms 'قديم' (eternal) and 'حال' (state).
2
Understand the logical dilemma
From the premise that the eternal's states are similar, identify the two possible outcomes: either nothing issues from it, or it issues perpetually.
3
Grasp the impossibility of distinction
Consider why it is impossible for the state of 'ceasing' to be distinguished from the state of 'commencing' under uniformity. This is the key step.
4
Connect to the philosophers' conclusion
Reflect on how this argument supports the eternity of the world. If the First Cause is immutable, a temporal beginning cannot occur.
This argument is classic in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانياً : [ و بالجملة فأحوال القديم ، إذا كانت متشابهة فإما أن لا يوجد عنه شيء قط . و إما أن يوجد على الدوام . فأما أن يتميز حال الترك عن حال الشروع ، فهو محال ]
And secondly: [In summary, the states of the eternal, if they are similar, then either nothing issues from it at all, or it issues perpetually. But for the state of ceasing to be distinguished from the state of commencing—that is impossible.]
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This lesson presents Ghazali's second, more concise formulation of the philosophers' argument. He states: In summary (بالجملة), the states of the eternal (أحوال القديم) are all similar. Given that uniformity, there are only two possibilities: either nothing ever issues from it, or something issues from it perpetually (على الدوام). It is impossible for there to be a differentiation (تميز) between the state of not producing (الترك) and the state of beginning to produce (الشروع). Because if the states are identical, there is no basis for choosing one moment over another. Therefore, the world must either be eternal (perpetual) or non-existent; it cannot have a temporal beginning. This is a classic argument for the eternity of the world based on the immutability of the First Cause.

Key Terms
قديم
حال
تشابه
الترك
الشروع
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies God's power to create freely.
Correction: The argument is based on logical consistency of an immutable cause producing a temporal effect, not on denial of power.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's second formulation, what are the only two possibilities if the states of the eternal are similar?
A) Either nothing issues from it, or it issues perpetually.
B) Either it issues sometimes, or it issues often.
C) Either it issues temporally, or it issues eternally.
D) Either it issues from itself, or from another.

Key Takeaway
The uniformity of the eternal's states necessitates that either nothing ever occurs or occurrence is perpetual, making a temporal beginning logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God as though He is 'stuck' in His ways, unable to respond to your specific situation because He is unchanging? How does this lesson's logical dilemma challenge or affirm your view of His freedom?
?
If the eternal's states are truly uniform, then your prayer cannot influence when God acts. Do you believe that your supplication actually makes a difference in timing? What does that imply about God's state before and after your prayer?
?
The philosophers used this argument to deny a temporal beginning. Have you ever doubted that God could bring something new into existence that was not already 'eternally' caused? Examine your heart: do you live as if God's power is limited by logic or as if He can truly act in time?

Ghazali's Third Proof: Eliminating Four Explanations
Ghazali asks why the world was not created earlier and rejects four explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects explanations based on divine inability, impossibility of the world, a new purpose, or a new instrument.

Core Idea
The eternal cannot change; therefore these explanations fail.

Ghazali presents his third and most precise formulation of the argument. He begins with a simple question: why did the world not exist before it did? He then systematically eliminates four possible answers, each of which would imply a change in the eternal—something impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and first two rejections
Read the Arabic passage up to 'وكلاهما محال'. Understand that Ghazali first rules out inability (عجز) and impossibility (استحالة) because they would require change in God or the world.
Notice the parallel structure: لا يمكن أن يحال على... ولا على...
2
Identify the third and fourth rejections
Continue reading to see the rejection of a new purpose (غرض) and a new instrument (آلة). Each would imply change in God.
3
Reflect on why each explanation implies change
For each explanation, ask: what would need to change in the eternal for this explanation to work? The answer reveals why Ghazali rejects it.
This connects to the core idea: the eternal cannot change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثاً : [ وتحقيقه أن يقال : لم لم يحدث العالم قبل حدوثه ؟ لا يمكن أن يحال ذلك على عجزه عن الأحداث ، ولا على استحالة الحدوث ؛ فإن ذلك يؤدى إلى أن ينقلب القديم من العجز إلى القدرة ، والعالم من الاستحالة إلى الإمكان ، وكلاهما محال . ولا يمكن أن يقال : لم يكن قبله غرض ثم تجدد غرض . ولا يمكن أن يحال على فقد آلة ، ثم على وجودها بل أقرب ما يتخيل أن يقال : لم يرد وجوده قبل ذلك ، فيلزم أن يقال : حصل وجوده لأنه صار مريداً لوجوده بعد أن لم يكن مريداً ، فتكون قد حدثت الإرادة . وحدوثه فى ذاته محال ؛ لأنه ليس محل الحوادث . وحدوثه لا في ذاته لا يجعله مريداً . ولنترك النظر في محل حدوثه ، أليس الإشكال قائماً في أصل حدوثه ؟ وأنه من أين حدث ؟ ولم حدث الآن ولم يحدث قبله ؟ أحدث الآن لا من جهة الله ؟ فإن جاز حادث من غير محدث ، فليكن العالم حادثاً لا صانع له ، و إلا فأى فرق بين حادث وحادث ؟ ، و إن حدث باحداث الله، فلم حدث الآن ولم يحدث قبل ؟ ألعدم آلة أو قدرة ، أو غرض ، أو طبيعة ، فلما أن تبدل ذلك بالوجود حدث ؟ عاد الإشكال بعينه أو لعدم الإرادة ؟ فتفتقر الإرادة إلى إرادة ، وكذا الإرادة الأولى و يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية . فإذن تحقق بالقول المطلق ، أن صدور الحادث من القديم ، من غير تغير أمر من القديم - في قدرة ، أو آلة ، أو وقت ، أو غرض ، أو طبع – محال
And thirdly: [Its verification is that it may be said: Why did the world not occur before its occurrence? That cannot be attributed to His inability to cause events, nor to the impossibility of occurrence, for that would lead to the eternal changing from inability to ability, and the world from impossibility to possibility—both are impossible. Nor can it be said that there was no purpose before and then a purpose arose. Nor can it be attributed to the lack of an instrument, then to its existence. Rather, the closest thing to imagine is to say: He did not will its existence before that, so it must be said: Its existence occurred because He became willing of its existence after He was not willing, so a will would have occurred. And its occurrence in His essence is impossible because He is not a locus for temporal events. And its occurrence not in His essence does not make Him willing. Let us leave aside the consideration of its locus of occurrence—is not the problem still standing regarding the very occurrence of the will? That it occurred? And from where did it occur? And why did it occur now and not before? Did it occur now not from God? For if a temporal event is allowed without an originator, then let the world be temporal without a maker; otherwise, what difference between one temporal event and another? And if it occurs by God's causing, then why did it occur now and not before? Was it due to the absence of an instrument, or power, or purpose, or nature, and when that changed to existence it occurred? The same problem returns. Or due to the absence of will? Then the will would need another will, and likewise the first will, leading to an infinite regress. So it is established, in absolute terms, that the emanation of the temporal from the eternal, without any change in the eternal—in power, instrument, time, purpose, or nature—is impossible.]
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This lesson presents Ghazali's third and most detailed formulation of the argument, followed by the overall conclusion. Ghazali begins by asking: Why did the world not exist before it did? He systematically eliminates five possible explanations: (1) It cannot be due to God's inability, for that would imply change from incapability to capability. (2) It cannot be due to the impossibility of the world's existence, for that would imply a change from impossibility to possibility. (3) It cannot be attributed to the emergence of a new purpose, as that would imply a new purpose arising in God. (4) It cannot be attributed to the acquisition of an instrument, as that would involve change. (5) The only remaining possibility is that God's will to create arose at that moment. But this leads to a problem: the will itself is a temporal event (حادث). If it occurs in God's essence, that is impossible because God is not a locus for temporal events. If it occurs outside His essence, it does not make Him a willing agent. Moreover, the same question applies to that will: why now? If we say the will came from God, then why did He will at that time? This leads to an infinite regress of wills. Ghazali then concludes: 'It is established, in absolute terms, that the emanation of the temporal from the eternal, without any change in the eternal—in power, instrument, time, purpose, or nature—is impossible.' This is the overall conclusion of the three proofs.

Key Terms
عجز
قدرة
غرض
آلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could have been incapable or that the world was impossible before, and then changed.
Correction: Ghazali shows both would require change in the eternal, which is impossible. The eternal cannot change from one state to another.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why cannot the world's delayed creation be due to God's previous inability?
A) Because inability is impossible for God.
B) Because it would require God to change from inability to ability.
C) Because the world was always possible.
D) Because God's power is limited.

Key Takeaway
The temporal world's delayed creation cannot be explained by any change in the eternal, so four common explanations are eliminated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a delay in something you deeply desire, do you ever secretly attribute it to God's inability or lack of care? How does Ghazali's elimination of these explanations challenge your heart to trust that God's unchanging power and purpose are at work even now?
?
Have you ever assumed that God's purpose for your life changed because your circumstances did? How does the impossibility of a new purpose in God reshape your understanding of His decree and your response to trials?
?
If God's attributes are eternally unchanging, what does that demand from you today regarding reliance on His strength and wisdom, not just in past moments but in your present situation?

Ghazali's Third Proof: The Will Explanation and Infinite Regress
Ghazali refutes the will explanation and concludes the overall argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing the world's creation to a new will in God leads to an infinite regress and is therefore untenable, and you will grasp the overall conclusion.

Core Idea
A new will in God is itself a temporal event requiring explanation.

After eliminating four explanations, Ghazali turns to the most plausible one: that God willed the world at that moment. But this new will is a temporal event. He then shows that this leads to an infinite chain of wills, which is impossible. The argument concludes with the fundamental principle that a temporal effect cannot arise from an eternal cause without change.

Guided Path
1
Read the will explanation and its initial problem
Read the passage from 'بل أقرب ما يتخيل' to 'لا يجعله مريداً'. Understand that Ghazali first considers the explanation of a new will, then points out that this will is a temporal event (حادث) that cannot occur in God's essence.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فتكون قد حدثت الإرادة'.
2
Follow the regress argument
Continue reading from 'ولنترك النظر' to 'يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية'. Ghazali shows that even if we leave aside where the will occurs, the will itself needs an explanation for its timing, leading to an infinite regress of wills.
3
Understand the conclusion
Read the final sentence. Ghazali concludes that a temporal effect from an eternal cause without any change is impossible. This is the overall result of the three proofs.
The conclusion mentions all five categories: power, instrument, time, purpose, nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's third proof, what happens if we attribute the world's creation to a new will in God?
A) The will itself becomes a temporal event requiring an explanation, leading to infinite regress.
B) The will is eternal and resolves the problem.
C) God's will changes from non-willing to willing, which is possible.
D) The will exists in God's essence without problem.

Key Takeaway
A temporal will in an eternal being is impossible, so the world's creation cannot be explained by a change in God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will cannot change or have a beginning, what does that mean for the times you've assumed that God's decision about your life 'changed' because of your dua? How do you need to adjust your understanding of God's decree? Will you commit to trusting His eternal wisdom even when you don't see the timing?
?
The philosophers thought a new will in God could explain creation. Have you ever thought of God's will as something that 'responds' to events in time? How does Ghazali's proof expose that as incoherent? What does this demand of you when you pray—do you pray with the conviction that God's will is eternal and unchanging, or do you seek to 'change' His mind?
?
Ghazali shows that any explanation of the world's origin that requires a change in God leads to absurdity. In your own life, where are you tempted to demand an explanation from God—something that would require Him to 'change' His plan to suit your timeline? How does this lesson call you to submit to His eternal order without demanding reasons? Will you repent of that attitude today?

The Third Category: Philosophical Claims Affecting Religion
Ghazali defines the third category and lists its examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali distinguishes the third category of philosophical claims that touch fundamental principles of Islam and why only these merit refutation in the Tahafut.

Core Idea
Ghazali refutes only philosophical claims that threaten core Islamic doctrines.

In this passage, Ghazali narrows the focus of his critique. Not every philosophical error must be refuted — only those that strike at the foundations of religion. He identifies three key doctrines that the philosophers denied, setting the agenda for the rest of the Tahafut.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style of Ghazali.
The passage is a single sentence but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the three examples
Ghazali gives three examples of fundamental principles: the origination of the world, the attributes of the Creator, and the resurrection of bodies. Find these in the Arabic.
These correspond to the subtopics: divine existence, divine attributes, and sam'iyyat.
3
Understand the philosophers' denial
Ghazali states that the philosophers have denied all of these. This sets up the need for refutation.
4
Reflect on the scope of refutation
Ghazali concludes that only this type (and its likes) should be shown corrupt, not other philosophical claims.
This guards against the misunderstanding that all philosophical views must be refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث : يتعلق النزاع فيه بأصل من أصول الدين ، كالقول في حدوث العالم ، وصفات الصانع ، وبيان حشر الأجساد والأبدان ؛ وقد أنكروا ذلك جميعاً فهذا الفن ونظائره هو الذى ينبغى أن يظهر فساد مذهبهم فيه دون ما عداه
The third section: the dispute in it concerns a fundamental principle of religion, such as the statement about the origination of the world, the attributes of the Maker, and the demonstration of the resurrection of bodies and forms; and they have denied all of that. So this type and its likes are what should be shown to be corrupt in their doctrine, not other than it.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines the third category of philosophical claims: those that touch a fundamental principle (asl) of religion. Ghazali gives three examples: the origination (huduth) of the world, the attributes (sifat) of the Creator, and the bodily resurrection (hashr al-ajsad wa al-abdan). He notes that the philosophers have denied all of these. Therefore, Ghazali concludes, it is this type and its likes (such as other essential doctrines) that must be refuted and shown to be false, and nothing beyond them. This sets the scope of the Tahafut: only the most essential issues warrant refutation.

Key Terms

حدوث العالم
صفات الصانع
حشر الأجساد والأبدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all philosophical views must be refuted.
Correction: Ghazali limits refutation to those affecting core doctrines (usul al-din), not other philosophical claims.

Checkpoint

A) The origination of the world
B) The attributes of the Creator
C) The resurrection of bodies
D) The interpretation of dreams

Key Takeaway
Ghazali restricts his refutation to philosophical claims that contradict fundamental Islamic doctrines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If you were to compile a shortlist of non-negotiable beliefs that you would defend at all costs, would Ghazali's three examples suffice? Why or why not? How does your personal list affect the way you engage with those who disagree with you?

The World's Creation: Shape is Irrelevant
Ghazali clarifies that only the world's origination matters, not its shape or structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the shape and structure of the world irrelevant to proving its creation by God.

Core Idea
The world's origin matters; its shape is just a detail.

Ghazali here focuses on the core theological question: is the world created or eternal? He argues that once we establish creation, the specific form—sphere, hexagon, or layers upon layers—is as trivial as counting onion layers. This lesson will help you grasp the methodological point that theological investigation centers on divine action, not cosmic architecture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: hadith (originated) and qadim (eternal).
2
Identify the essential question
The passage states the investigation is only about whether the world is originated or eternal. Pause to see that this is the main theological debate.
3
Appreciate the analogy
Ghazali compares detailed cosmic speculation to counting onion layers. Reflect on why such details are irrelevant to proving God's act of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا لأن البحث في العالم كونه حادثاً أو قديماً ، ثم إذا ثبت حدوثه فسواء كان كرة ، أو بسيطاً أو مسدساً ، أم مثمناً ؛ وسواء كانت السموات وما تحتها ثلاث عشرة طبقة - كما قالوه - أو أقل أو أكثر ؛ فنسبة النظر فيه إلى البحث الإلهى ، كنسبة النظر فى طبقات البصلة وعددها وعدد حب الرمان ؛ فالمقصود كونه من فعل الله فقط كيفما كان
And this is because the investigation into the world is only about whether it is originated or eternal. Then if its origination is established, then it is the same whether it is a sphere, or a flat surface, or hexagonal, or octagonal; and whether the heavens and what is beneath them are thirteen layers—as they said—or less or more; the relation of considering this to theological investigation is like the relation of considering the layers of an onion and their number and the number of pomegranate seeds; the purpose is only that it is an act of God, no matter how it is.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a methodological clarification explaining what truly matters in theological discussions about the world. The author states that the only essential question is whether the world is created (hadith) or eternal (qadim). Once its createdness is established, the specific shape or structure—whether spherical, flat, hexagonal, octagonal, or the number of celestial layers—is irrelevant. He uses a striking analogy: investigating such details about the world is like counting the layers of an onion or the seeds of a pomegranate—trivial and unrelated to the theological purpose. The ultimate point is that the world is a creation of God, regardless of its form.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you examine your own life, do you focus more on irrelevant details than on your relationship with the Creator? What will you change today?
?
If the only thing that matters is that the world is an act of God, then everything you see—every atom—is a sign of His action. Do you live as if this is true? When you look at the world, do you see God's hand, or do you get lost in counting 'onion layers'? Make a decision to start seeing His act in everything.
?
This lesson says the purpose is only that the world is an act of God. Is that your purpose in studying? Or are you just adding to your knowledge? Ask yourself: If you stripped away all the details of theology, would you still be standing in awe of God as the Creator? If not, what has gone wrong? Commit to reading with that purpose.

The Twenty Questions: A Map of Ghazali's Refutations
Ghazali enumerates the twenty questions he will refute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope and structure of Ghazali's twenty questions in the Tahafut, which serve as an index of his refutations of the philosophers.

Core Idea
Ghazali's twenty questions map the key battlegrounds between faith and philosophy.

Ghazali opens the body of his work by providing an index of twenty questions (masa'il) that will be treated in the book. This list not only outlines the topics but also reveals his method: each question is a point where he claims to have exposed a contradiction (tanāquḍ) in the philosophers' doctrines. Before diving into the intricate arguments, it is essential to have this roadmap in mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the List Carefully
Read through the Arabic and English list to get a sense of the range of topics. Notice the phrasing: 'refutation of...', 'incapacitating them from...', 'showing that...'.
The list is an index; each item will be elaborated later.
2
Identify the Pattern
Observe that Ghazali alternates between refutations of specific doctrines and demonstrations of the philosophers' incapacity to prove their claims.
This reflects his dialectical approach.
3
Group by Theme
The questions can be roughly grouped: cosmology (1-5), divine attributes and knowledge (6-13), celestial souls (14-16), miracles (17), and human soul (18).
The grouping helps see the structure.
4
Reflect on the Order
Notice the sequence moves from the world's eternity to divine unity, attributes, knowledge, then to celestial and human realms.
This order builds a comprehensive critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنذكر الآن بعد المقدمات
فهرست المسائل
التي أظهرنا تناقض مذهبهم فيها في هذا الكتاب وهى عشرون مسألة :
المسألة الأولى : إبطال مذهبهم فى أزلية العالم .
الثانية : إبطال مذهبهم في أبدية العالم .
الثالثة : بيان تلبيسهم فى قولهم : إن الله صانع العالم، وإن العالم صنعه .
الرابعة : في تعجيزهم عن إثبات الصانع .
الخامسة : تعجيزهم عن إقامة الدليل على استحالة إلهين في

السابعة : في إبطال قولهم : إن ذات الأول لا تنقسم بالجنس والفصل .
الثامنة : في إبطال قولهم : إن الأول موجود بسيط بلا ماهية .
التاسعة : في تعجيزهم عن بيان أن الأول ليس بجسم .
العاشرة : في بيان أن القول بالدهر ونفى الصانع لازم لهم .
الحادية عشرة : في تعجيزهم عن القول بأن الأول يعلم غيره .

الرابعة عشرة : في قولهم : إن السماء حيوان متحرك بالإرادة .
الخامسة عشرة : فى إبطال ما ذكروه من الغرض المحرك للسماء .
السادسة عشرة : في إبطال قولهم : إن نفوس السماوات تعلم جميع الجزئيات.
السابعة عشرة : فى إبطال قولهم باستحالة خرق العادات .
الثامنة عشرة : في قولهم : إن نفس الإنسان جوهر قائم بنفسه ليس بجسم ولا عرض .
And let us now mention, after the introductions, the index of the questions in which we have shown the contradiction of their doctrine in this book, and they are twenty questions: The first question: Refutation of their doctrine on the eternity of the world. The second: Refutation of their doctrine on the everlastingness of the world. The third: Explanation of their obfuscation in their statement that God is the Maker of the world and that the world is His making. The fourth: On incapacitating them from proving the Maker. The fifth: Incapacitating them from establishing the proof of the impossibility of two gods in existence. The sixth: On refuting their doctrine in negating the attributes. The seventh: On refuting their statement that the First Essence (dhat al-Awwal) does not divide by genus and difference. The eighth: On refuting their statement that the First is a simple existent without quiddity (mahiyya). The ninth: On incapacitating them from showing that the First is not a body. The tenth: On showing that the doctrine of (purely) time (dahr) and denial of the Maker is necessary for them. The eleventh: On incapacitating them from the statement that the First knows other than Himself. The twelfth: On incapacitating them from the statement that He knows His essence. The thirteenth: On refuting their statement that the First does not know particulars. The fourteenth: On their statement that the heaven is an animal moving voluntarily. The fifteenth: On refuting what they mentioned of the purpose moving the heaven. The sixteenth: On refuting their statement that the souls of the heavens know all particulars. The seventeenth: On refuting their statement on the impossibility of breaking habits (khariq al-adat). The eighteenth: On their statement that the human soul is a self-subsistent substance (jawhar qa'im bi-nafsihi), not a body or an accident.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the twenty questions (masa'il) that Ghazali will address in the book to demonstrate contradictions in the philosophers' doctrines. The list is introduced after the preliminaries. Each question is presented with a brief description of the refutation or argument. The first two questions target the eternity (azaliyya) and everlastingness (abadiyya) of the world. The third exposes their obfuscation regarding God as Maker. Questions four and five aim to incapacitate them from proving the existence of the Maker and the impossibility of two gods. The sixth refutes their denial of divine attributes. Seven and eight attack their claims about the First's indivisibility into genus and species and its simple nature without quiddity. Nine challenges them to show the First is not a body. Ten shows that their view implies acceptance of 'dahr' (time) and denial of the Maker. Eleven and twelve aim to prove that the First knows others and His own essence. Thirteen refutes their claim that He does not know particulars. Fourteen discusses their statement that the heaven is an animal with will. Fifteen refutes their proposed purpose for the heaven's motion. Sixteen refutes that the souls of the heavens know all particulars. Seventeen refutes their claim that the breaking of natural habits (miracles) is impossible. Eighteen addresses their view that the human soul is a self-subsistent substance, immortal, not a body or accident. The list continues, but only the first eighteen appear in this window.

Three Philosophical Stances on the Eternity of the World
Ghazali enumerates three philosophical stances on the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main ancient philosophical positions on the eternity of the world as presented by Ghazali, and the interpretive debate surrounding Plato's view.

Core Idea
Ancient philosophers split on whether the world is eternal, created, or unknown.

Ghazali opens his refutation of the philosophers by first laying out their own internal disagreements. Before arguing against eternalism, he shows that not all philosophers agreed — some held creation, others suspended judgment. This passage sets the stage for his critique by mapping the spectrum of views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall sense of the three positions Ghazali presents.
2
Identify the three positions
As you re-read, clearly identify the majority view (eternal co-existence), Plato's reported view (origination), and Galen's suspension. Note how Ghazali distinguishes them.
Pay attention to the nuance: the majority view is not simple temporal priority but priority in essence.
3
Understand the concept of priority by essence
The majority philosophers describe God's priority over the world as 'priority by essence and rank, not by time'. Reflect on the cause-effect analogy (light and sun). This is key to their eternalism.
This concept will be central in the subsequent refutation.
4
Examine the footnote on Plato
Read the footnote by al-Jalāl al-Dawānī. It discusses the interpretation of Plato's statement. Understand the argument that Plato likely meant essential origination (ḥudūth dhātī), not temporal creation.
The footnote is a commentary on how some philosophers tried to reconcile Plato with eternalism.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Ask: Why does Ghazali begin with these disagreements? How does showing internal division among philosophers affect the strength of their position?
Ghazali uses their disagreement to undermine the claim of a unified philosophical doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفصيل لمذهب:
مسألة [ ١ ]
في إبطال قولهم بقدم العالم
اختلف الفلاسفة في قدم العالم؛ فالذي استقر عليه رأى جماهيرهم المتقدمين والمتأخرين، القول بقدمه، وأنه لم يزل موجوداً مع الله تعالى، ومعلولا له، ومساوقاً له، غير متأخر عنه بالزمان، مساوقة المعلول للعلة، ومساوقة النور للشمس، وأن تقدم البارى عليه، كتقدم العلة على المعلول، وهو تقدم بالذات والرتبة، لا بالزمان
وحكى عن أفلاطن أنه قال: العالم مكوّن ومحدث، ثم منهم من أوّل كلامه، وأبى أن يكون حدوث العالم معتقداً له (1).
وذهب جالينوس فى آخر عمره فى الكتاب الذي سماه « ما يعتقده جالينوس رأيا » إلى التوقف في هذه المسألة. وأنه لا يدرى: العالم قديم أو محدث؟ وربما
(1) أشار الجلال الدواني إلى الخلاف حول رأى أفلاطون فقال: « ونقل عن أفلاطون القول بحدوث العالم، فقيل إن مراده الحدوث الذاتى، وقد رأيت أنا كتاباً بخط واحد من الفلاسفة الإسلاميين، قد نسخ قبل هذا التاريخ - يشير إلى تاريخ زمنه - بأربعمائة سنة، ذكر فيه – نقلا عن أرسطاطاليس - أن الفلاسفة كلهم اتفقوا على قدم العالم، إلا رجلا واحدا منهم، وقال مصنف ذلك الكتاب: إن مراد أرسطو مى هذا الرجل، أفلاطون، فلا يمكن حمله على الحدوث الذاتى كما لا يخفى
وقوله: « فلا يمكن حمله على الحدوث الذاتى كما لا يخفى » يفسر بأن حمل قول أفلاطن على الحدوث الذاتي يجعل معنى العبارة هكذا: « اتفق الفلاسفة على قدم العالم إلا أفلاطون فإنه قال بحدوثه حدوثاً ذاتياً، فبمقتضى قاعدة الاستثناء يكون رأى الفلاسفة محمولا على القدم الذاتى وهو خلاف المروى عنهم ثم نقل الحدوث عنه مخالف لما اشتهر من قوله بقدم النفوس الإنسانية وقدم البعد المجرد »
Detailed exposition of the doctrine:
Issue [1]
On refuting their claim of the eternity of the world
The philosophers differed concerning the eternity of the world. That upon which the view of the majority of them, both ancients and moderns, settled is the claim of its eternity, and that it has never ceased to exist along with God Most High, and it is an effect of Him, and co-eternal with Him, not temporally posterior to Him, as the effect is co-eternal with the cause, and as light is co-eternal with the sun, and that the priority of the Creator over it is like the priority of the cause over the effect, which is priority in essence and rank, not in time.
And it is related that Plato said: The world is produced and originated. Then among them are those who interpreted his words and refused to consider the origination of the world as his belief.
And Galen in his later life, in the book which he named 'What Galen Believes as an Opinion', adopted suspension of judgment on this issue, and that he does not know whether the world is eternal or originated.
(1) Al-Jalāl al-Dawānī pointed to the disagreement concerning the opinion of Plato and said: 'It is transmitted that Plato held the origination of the world, and it is said that his meaning is essential origination. And I saw a book in the handwriting of one of the Islamic philosophers, copied before this date – he refers to his own time – by four hundred years, in which it is mentioned – quoting from Aristotle – that all the philosophers agreed on the eternity of the world except one man among them, and the author of that book said: The one Aristotle meant is Plato, so his statement cannot be interpreted as essential origination as is not hidden.
And his saying: "so his statement cannot be interpreted as essential origination as is not hidden" is explained by the fact that interpreting Plato's statement as essential origination would make the meaning of the phrase as: "The philosophers all agreed on the eternity of the world except Plato, for he held its origination as essential origination." Then by the rule of exception, the opinion of the philosophers would be understood as essential eternity, which contradicts what is reported from them, and then reporting origination from him contradicts what is well-known from his statement about the eternity of human souls and the eternity of the abstract dimension.'
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the first issue (Mas'ala 1) in the detailed exposition of the philosophers' doctrines. Ghazali presents the disagreement among the ancient philosophers on whether the world is eternal (qadīm) or originated (muḥdath). He first states the majority view: the world is eternal, co-existing with God as an effect co-exists with its cause, not temporally posterior. The priority of God is only in essence and rank, not in time. Then he notes that Plato reportedly said the world is produced (mukawwan) and originated (muḥdath), but some philosophers interpret his words to avoid attributing to him a belief in temporal creation. Finally, he mentions Galen's famous suspension of judgment (tawaqquf) – Galen said he does not know whether the world is eternal or originated. The footnote by al-Jalāl al-Dawānī adds a historical argument against interpreting Plato's statement as essential origination (ḥudūth dhātī) by citing a four-hundred-year-old manuscript that reports Aristotle as saying all philosophers except one agreed on eternity, and that one is Plato. This implies that Plato's view is not essential origination but rather a different type of origination (perhaps temporal) that would contradict his other statements about the eternity of souls and the abstract dimension. Thus, the footnote reinforces the difficulty in interpreting Plato's position and supports the view that Plato also held eternalism in some sense.

Key Terms
قدم العالم (qidam al-ʿālam)
حدوث العالم (ḥudūth al-ʿālam)
تقدم بالذات والرتبة (taqaddum bi al-dhāt wa al-rutba)
مساوقة (musāwaqa)
علة ومعلول (ʿilla wa maʿlūl)
أفلاطن (Aflāṭun)
جالينوس (Jālīnūs)
توقف (tawaqquf)
حدوث ذاتي (ḥudūth dhātī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All ancient philosophers agreed that the world is eternal.
Correction: This passage shows internal disagreement: Plato reportedly held the world is originated, and Galen suspended judgment. The philosophical tradition was not unanimous.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what was the view of the majority of philosophers on the world's origin?

Aquinas's Refutation of Emanation and Proof of Creation
Aquinas lays out his doctrine of creation by participation and refutes emanationist theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aquinas's arguments that all beings except God are created by participation, and why he rejects emanation, pantheism, and the idea that creatures can create.

Core Idea
Only God is self-subsistent; everything else exists by participation and requires infinite power to create.

Aquinas builds on the unique nature of God's existence. He argues that since only God is existence itself, everything else must be created. In this passage, he attacks several competing views—Neoplatonic emanation, pantheism, and Ibn Sina's emanationist principle—and shows why only God has the power to create.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing; then read the literal translation to grasp Aquinas's arguments.
Notice the logical structure: premise, refutations, conclusion.
2
Grasp the core premise
Understand the claim that only God is self-subsistent existence (الوجود القائم بذاته); all others exist by participation (بالمشاركة).
This is the foundation of Aquinas's creation doctrine.
3
Examine the refutations
Identify the three views Aquinas refutes: Neoplatonic emanation, pantheism, and Ibn Sina's 'from one only one' principle. Note his reasoning for each.
He uses the disanalogy between necessary and voluntary agents to refute Ibn Sina.
4
Understand the argument against creatures creating
Follow Aquinas's argument that creatures cannot create because they are finite and lack self-subsistent existence; only infinite power can bridge the gap from non-existence to existence.
This reinforces the exclusive divine prerogative to create.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل موجود - ما خلا الله - مخلوق من الله ضرورة؛ لأن الوجود القائم بذاته لا يمكن أن يكون إلا واحداً؛ فيلزم أن كل ما خلا الله ليس عين وجوده، ولكنه موجود بالمشاركة. وليس الوجود بالمشاركة صدوراً عن ذات الله، كما تقول الأفلاطونية الحديثة؛ لأن ما يصدر عن الذات صدوراً ضرورياً، فهو مثل الذات، وليس العالم مثل الله بالطبع. هذه الناحية أيضاً يسقط مذهب وحدة الوجود، الذي يعتبر العالم مظهراً لله. أما قول ابن سينا: إن من شأن الواحد دائماً أن يصدر عنه واحد، فيصدق على الفاعل، لا على الفاعل الإرادي الذي يفعل بالصورة المعقولة، ولما كان الله يتعقل أموراً كثيرة، فهو يقدر أن يفعل أشياء كثيرة. يضاف إلى ذلك استحالة صدور الموجودات بعضها عن بعض؛ لأن المخلوق غير موجود بذاته، فلا يستطيع أن يمنح وجوداً ليس له بالذات، ولئن كان المخلوق الموجود متناهياً، فإن المسافة بين اللاوجود والوجود لا متناهية، فالخلق يقتضى قدرة لا متناهية؛ لذلك كان خاصاً بالله وحده.
Every existent – except God – is necessarily created by God; because self-subsistent existence cannot be but one; so it follows that everything other than God is not identical with His existence, but exists by participation. And existence by participation is not an emanation from God's essence, as the Neoplatonists say; because what emanates from the essence necessarily is similar to the essence, and the world is not similar to God, of course. From this aspect also the doctrine of pantheism falls, which considers the world a manifestation of God. As for Ibn Sina's statement: 'It is the property of the One that only one proceeds from it,' it is true of the agent, not of the voluntary agent who acts by an intelligible form. Since God intelligizes many things, He is able to do many things. Added to that is the impossibility of some existents proceeding from others; because the creature does not exist by itself, so it cannot grant an existence that it does not possess by itself. And if the created existent is finite, then the distance between non-existence and existence is infinite; therefore creation requires infinite power; thus it is exclusive to God alone.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents Saint Thomas Aquinas's argument for creation. It begins with the principle that only God is self-subsistent existence, so all other beings exist by participation (مشاركة). Aquinas then refutes Neoplatonic emanation, because emanation would produce something like God, but the world is unlike God. He also refutes pantheism. Next, he addresses Ibn Sina's principle that from one only one proceeds, distinguishing necessary agents from voluntary agents: God acts voluntarily by intellectual forms, so He can create many things. Finally, Aquinas argues that creatures cannot create others because they lack self-subsistence and are finite; creation requires infinite power, belonging only to God.

Key Terms
الوجود القائم بذاته
الوجود بالمشاركة
الصدور
الفاعل الإرادي
قدرة لا متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation happens by emanation from God's essence, and creatures can also create.
Correction: Aquinas argues that emanation would make the world like God (which is false), and that creatures cannot create because they lack self-subsistence and infinite power.

Checkpoint
Why does Aquinas argue that creatures cannot create other existents?
A) Because they are finite and lack self-subsistent existence
B) Because they have no will
C) Because they are evil
D) Because God forbids it

Key Takeaway
Only God possesses self-subsistent existence and infinite power, so He alone can create; all other beings exist by participation and are created by Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you relying on your own finite power or recognizing the Creator? Identify one area where you are acting as if you are the ultimate cause and surrender it to God today.
?
The passage rejects that anything can give existence except God.
